RESOURCE FILE 4

The Meta Model in Brief

REMEMBER, THE META MODEL is designed to separate out what part of a person‚Äôs model works from what doesn‚Äôt. Even though the patterns are laid out here under the headings of Deletion, Distortion, and Generalization, always go for the biggest chunk question available to you. ‚ÄúBiggest chunk‚Äù refers to the question that will give you the maximum amount of information in the shortest possible time. The most useful question is usually, ‚ÄúHow do you know?‚Äù To answer this, the subject has to use the language of process, rather than of content (story).

Note, too, that even though we sometimes refer to the questions we use to recover lost information, we need to avoid sounding like interrogators.

DELETIONS

Simple Deletion. Information is left out of the statement.

Example: ‚ÄúI‚Äôm anxious.‚Äù

Question(s): ‚ÄúHow do you know you‚Äôre anxious?‚Äù ‚ÄúHow do you know you‚Äôre not really excited?‚Äù ‚ÄúWhat actually happens that lets you know you‚Äôre depressed?‚Äù

Unspecified Referential Index. The subject of the statement is unspecified.

Example: ‚ÄúThey just don‚Äôt like me.‚Äù

Question(s): ‚ÄúWho specifically doesn‚Äôt like you?‚Äù ‚ÄúHow do you know they don‚Äôt like you?‚Äù

Comparative Deletion. A comparison is made, but it is not clear as to who or what is being compared. Be alert for words such as least, most, more, less, better, worse.

Example: ‚ÄúThe way we‚Äôre doing it is better.‚Äù

Question(s): ‚ÄúWhat will let us know it‚Äôs better?‚Äù ‚ÄúBetter than what?‚Äù ‚ÄúBetter than whom?‚Äù

Unspecified Verb. The author or agent of an action is unclear.

Example: ‚ÄúIt‚Äôs causing problems in my marriage.‚Äù

Question(s): ‚ÄúWho/how/what specifically?‚Äù ‚ÄúHow do you know?‚Äù

Nominalization. A process (verb) has been turned into a ‚Äúthing.‚Äù Nominalizations are abstract nouns. They have no physical existence in the world. European languages favor nouns over verbs with the result that many processes are perceived as being ‚Äúset in stone,‚Äù rather than as fluid events in motion. The test for a nominalization is: ‚ÄúCan I put it in a box‚Äîalbeit a large box?‚Äù Examples of nominalizations include love, relationship, respect, truth, communication, freedom, anxiety, depression, etc. Questions are intended to restore process to the ‚Äústuck‚Äù state.

Example: ‚ÄúMy relationship is in trouble.‚Äù

Question(s): ‚ÄúWhat about the way in which you are relating is troubling you?‚Äù ‚ÄúHow do you know the way you are relating is causing you to feel troubled?‚Äù

DISTORTIONS

Mind reading. The speaker claims to know, or acts as if he knows, what another person or people think, feel, or believe.

Example: ‚ÄúWhen I get up to speak, people will be critical of me.‚Äù

Question(s): ‚ÄúHow do you know?‚Äù ‚ÄúWhat makes you think they‚Äôre not just considering what you say?‚Äù

Lost Performative. A value judgment is made, without stating who has made the judgment.

Example: ‚ÄúRight-thinking people agree that pornography is bad.‚Äù

Question(s): ‚ÄúRight-thinking according to whom?‚Äù ‚ÄúHow do you know they are right-thinking?‚Äù ‚ÄúHow do you know they know it‚Äôs bad?‚Äù

Cause-Effect. A particular action is taken to cause a specific response or reaction. Listen for words such as: because, if/then, makes, drives, compels, causes.

Example: ‚ÄúThe way she looks at me drives me crazy.‚Äù

Question(s): ‚ÄúHow specifically does the way she look at you drive you crazy?‚Äù ‚ÄúHow do you know to feel crazy when she looks at you?‚Äù ‚ÄúWhat happens exactly when she looks at you that makes you feel crazy?‚Äù

Complex Equivalence. In this situation one action, experience, or behavior is taken to mean another without explanation or proof. Listen for words such as: means, therefore, implies.

Example: ‚ÄúHis e-mail was so brief, he must be angry with me.‚Äù

Question(s): ‚ÄúHow do you know a short e-mail means he‚Äôs angry with you?‚Äù ‚ÄúMight he not have been very busy rather than angry?‚Äù ‚ÄúHave you ever dashed off a particularly short e-mail? Did that automatically mean you were angry with the person you were sending it to?‚Äù

Presupposition. An assumption or assumptions (unstated in the sentence), taken to be present or true for the sentence to be understood.

Example: ‚ÄúWhen are you going to start to show your affection?‚Äù The presuppositions include: that you are not showing your affection, that you could show affection (if you so desired), that there is some kind of relationship in which showing affection is appropriate. Answering the wrong part of the statement will often worsen the communication problems.

Question(s): ‚ÄúHow do you know I‚Äôm not showing affection?‚Äù ‚ÄúWhat needs to happen that will let you know I am showing affection?‚Äù

GENERALIZATIONS

Universal Quantifiers. Universal quantifiers are very commonly encountered when people feel stuck or disoriented. They imply there is no exception to their experience. Listen for words such as: always, never, every, all, everyone, no one, everything, nothing.

Example: ‚ÄúI‚Äôm always depressed.‚Äù

Question(s): Either exaggerate the generalization to lighten the effect, or use counterexamples. ‚ÄúWhat? Always? Even in the shower? Even when you‚Äôre asleep?‚Äù ‚ÄúYou laughed a little earlier today. You didn‚Äôt seem depressed then.‚Äù

Modal Operators of Necessity or Probability. Modal operators of necessity suggest something is required or not required to happen. Listen for words such as: must, mustn‚Äôt, should, shouldn‚Äôt, need to, have to, etc. Modal operators of possibility include words such as: can, can‚Äôt, possible, impossible, will, won‚Äôt, may, may not. Modal operators become problematic when they limit volition.

Example: ‚ÄúI can‚Äôt get started in the mornings.‚Äù

Question(s): Restore volition and challenge the limitation. ‚ÄúSo, you‚Äôre telling me you have never been able to get started in the mornings?‚Äù ‚ÄúWhat would happen if you did get started?‚Äù ‚ÄúWhat would happen if you didn‚Äôt?‚Äù ‚ÄúWhat prevents you from getting started?‚Äù ‚ÄúHow do you know when you can‚Äôt get started?‚Äù

RESOURCE FILE 5

Milton Model Patterns

THE MILTON MODEL HYPNOTIC language patterns pace the listener‚Äôs experience by the simple process of allowing him to supply meaning to the statements from his own, rather than another‚Äôs, experience. This requires that the language be ‚Äúartfully vague‚Äù‚Äîbut systematically so. The Milton Model is sometimes described as the ‚Äúmirror image‚Äù of the Meta Model, but it includes several environments not relevant to the latter. Since the patterns move the listener into higher levels of thought and more introspective states of mind, they are naturally trance-inducing.

1. MIND READING

Claiming to know someone else‚Äôs thoughts or feelings without specifying how you acquired that information.

Examples:

‚ÄúI know you‚Äôre the kind of person who wants to learn how to go into trance.‚Äù

‚ÄúMany people feel as you do that things can only get better.‚Äù

‚ÄúI know you believe this is going to be difficult, but it‚Äôs worth it.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou realize how much of the way you feel is within your control.‚Äù

2. LOST PERFORMATIVE

Value judgments that omit identifying the person doing the judging.

Examples:

‚ÄúRelaxing is good once you know how.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt is good to know that things are getting easier.‚Äù

‚ÄúOne thing we know is that communication is a learnable skill.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt is a known fact that people like people who are like themselves.‚Äù

3. CAUSE AND EFFECT

Statements implying that a particular action causes a specific reaction.

Examples:

‚ÄúRealizing you have a problem is part of the way you fix it.‚Äù

‚ÄúSeeing her expression makes me angry.‚Äù

‚ÄúLearning about NLP will make you a great communicator.‚Äù

‚ÄúBy coming here, you will be able to learn many skills.‚Äù

4. COMPLEX EQUIVALENCE

Suggests that one thing is related to and means something else. This may or may not be true.

Examples:

‚ÄúBeing here means that you will change easily.‚Äù

‚ÄúYour hand is coming down. That means you are going deeper into trance.‚Äù

‚ÄúYour face is softening. You must be starting to relax.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou have chosen the most comfortable chair; therefore, you will go even more deeply into trance.‚Äù

5. PRESUPPOSITION

Something that is unstated but is assumed to be present and true for the statement to be understood.

Temporal presuppositions are words suggesting the passage or importance of time (e.g., when, after, during, before, while).

Examples:

‚ÄúWhen you close your eyes, you will begin to relax.‚Äù

‚ÄúAfter you‚Äôve taken some time out to relax, I want you to consider what you will be doing next.‚Äù

‚ÄúAs you go into trance, pay attention to the difference in feeling between your left hand and your right hand.‚Äù

Ordinal presuppositions sequence the listener‚Äôs experience by using numbers and position.

Examples:

‚ÄúJust notice which part of your body feels more comfortable first.‚Äù

‚ÄúThink about what will happen after that‚Ä¶and just after that‚Ä¶‚Äù

‚ÄúThe next thing that will probably happen is that your hands begin to feel warm.‚Äù

6. UNIVERSAL QUANTIFIER

Universal quantifiers imply there are no exceptions to the current experience.

Examples:

‚ÄúEveryone has had the experience of going into trance, even though they might not have recognized it as trance.‚Äù

‚ÄúNo one need know your secret of staying calm in demanding circumstances.‚Äù

‚ÄúEach time you breathe out, you become a little more relaxed.‚Äù

7. MODAL OPERATORS OF NECESSITY OF POSSIBILITY

Modal operators of necessity suggest something is required or not required to happen.

Examples:

‚ÄúYou needn‚Äôt even bother trying to relax.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou can easily notice how much your whole system has already begun to settle down.‚Äù

‚ÄúWe don‚Äôt have to try to go into trance; we simply let it happen in its own way.‚Äù

8. NOMINALIZATION

Processes presented as ‚Äúthings.‚Äù Verbs turned into nouns.

Examples (italicized):

‚ÄúAs you drift deeper, you can come to a new understanding.‚Äù

‚ÄúTrance is a naturally occurring phenomenon.‚Äù

‚ÄúYour unconscious will help you make new learnings.‚Äù

9. UNSPECIFIED VERB

Implies action without describing how the action has occurred or will occur.

Examples:

‚ÄúYou can imagine how things will be better.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou can begin to make those changes now.‚Äù

‚ÄúWe can get through this to a better relationship.‚Äù

10. TAG QUESTION

A question added at the end of a statement/question, designed to increase compliance. Even though it is formulated as a question, it is downwardly inflected, like a statement or command. The effect is either strengthened or weakened according to where the tag question is placed in the sentence.

Examples:

‚ÄúAs you stare at that spot on the ceiling, your vision begins to change, does it not?‚Äù (strong)

‚ÄúPeople can, can they not, decide to make important changes?‚Äù (weaker)

‚ÄúWouldn‚Äôt you prefer to close your eyes and rest now?‚Äù (weakest)

11. LACK OF REFERENTIAL INDEX

The sentence fails to specify who is the agent or object of an action.

Examples:

‚ÄúPeople can change more easily than they think.‚Äù

‚ÄúOne can very soon feel the effects of exercising regularly.‚Äù

‚ÄúMeditation is good for those who don‚Äôt need a very directive experience of trance.‚Äù

12. COMPARATIVE DELETION (UNSPECIFIED COMPARISON)

A comparison that is made without specifying who or what is being compared.

Examples:

‚ÄúYou will start to notice feeling better.‚Äù

‚ÄúEven if you try harder you are unlikely to succeed.‚Äù

‚ÄúMore people are starting to accept hypnosis as the useful tool that it is.‚Äù

13. PACE CURRENT EXPERIENCE

Using sensory-grounded, behaviorally specific information to describe current experience.

Examples:

‚ÄúYou are sitting in the chair‚Ä¶

‚Äú‚Ä¶with your feet on the floor‚Ä¶

‚Äú‚Ä¶and your hands in your lap, as‚Ä¶

‚Äú‚Ä¶you start to relax‚Ä¶‚Äù

14. DOUBLE BIND

Also known as the ‚Äúillusion of choice.‚Äù Two statements that appear to give the subject a choice, although either will meet the speaker‚Äôs intention.

Examples:

‚ÄúWould you like to start now, or a little later?‚Äù

‚ÄúDo you want to sit in this chair or the other one to go into trance?‚Äù

‚ÄúYou may notice changes immediately, or in a day or two. The important thing is to be alert to what‚Äôs different and better.‚Äù

15. EMBEDDED COMMANDS

A command that forms part of a larger sentence. The command is subtly ‚Äúanalog marked‚Äù to alert the listener‚Äôs unconscious to its importance. This may be done with a change in volume, tonality, or body language.

Examples:

‚ÄúSo looking at needles doesn‚Äôt make you feel comfortable now?‚Äù

‚ÄúWe are not saying that change is easy.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs good that you‚Äôve decided to become a nonsmoker.‚Äù

16. CONVERSATIONAL POSTULATE

A ‚Äúrhetorical question‚Äù that, if taken literally, would require a response or action. Conversational Postulates may also include Embedded Commands.

Examples:

‚ÄúCan you set aside the worries of the day and just put your feet up and relax?‚Äù

‚ÄúCould you close the door and take a seat?‚Äù

‚ÄúIs it possible to choose to change the way you‚Äôve been communicating with your children?‚Äù

17. EXTENDED QUOTE

A succession of quotes designed to create mild confusion in the listener, increase suggestibility and compliance, and embed process instructions or commands.

Examples:

‚ÄúSome time ago, when I was teaching a seminar in Oakland, one of the delegates said to me, ‚ÄòYou know, my grandfather had very similar ideas. He said, ‚ÄúYou don‚Äôt have to struggle to achieve things. I always tell people, ‚ÄòKnow what you want and find the easiest way of getting it,‚Äô‚Äù and he said he always found that planning is the best way. I always remember the day he said, ‚Äú‚ÄòIf it isn‚Äôt easy, it isn‚Äôt right.‚Äô‚Äù

18. SELECTIONAL RESTRICTION VIOLATION

Attributing intelligence or feelings to inanimate objects.

Examples:

‚ÄúYour chair supports you in becoming more relaxed.‚Äù

‚ÄúThe symptom is saying, ‚ÄòIt‚Äôs time to change.‚Äô‚Äù

‚ÄúYour outcomes want other people to change, not you.‚Äù

19. AMBIGUITY

Words or statements that have more than one meaning. Several deep structures for a single surface structure, prompting transderivational search on the part of the listener.

Phonological Ambiguity (written differently, but sounds the same).

Examples:

‚Äúyour unconscious‚Äù/‚Äúyou‚Äôre unconscious‚Äù

‚Äúdelight at the end of the tunnel‚Äù/‚Äúthe light at the end of the tunnel‚Äù

‚Äúby now‚Äù/‚Äúbuy now‚Äù

a part/apart, I/eye, heel/heal, know/no, see/sea, write/right, not/knot, hole/whole.

Syntactic Ambiguity (syntactic function of word or phrase cannot easily be determined from the utterance).

Examples:

‚ÄúHypnotizing hypnotists can be tricky.‚Äù

‚ÄúSpeaking to you as a person determined to change.‚Äù

‚ÄúThe problems are caused by visiting relatives.‚Äù

Scope Ambiguity (context is not clear as to which part of a sentence verbs or modifier apply).

Examples:

‚ÄúThe disturbing thoughts and feelings‚Äù (are the feelings also disturbing?)

‚ÄúThe long nights and days‚Äù (are the days also long?)

Punctuation Ambiguity (well-formed sentences joined by a word or phrase to create an ill-formed sentence. This prompts confusion and transderivational search in the listener).

Examples (link word italicized):

‚ÄúI like your watch how your breathing begins to slow down.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou can learn how to relax each muscle in your body.‚Äù

‚ÄúI like your giggle when the good feeling starts to spin.‚Äù

20. UTILIZATION

Pacing the subject by incorporating the entirety of his or her experience, internal and external. Whatever happens can be utilized as part of the process.

Examples:

Client 1: ‚ÄúI haven‚Äôt noticed any changes.‚Äù

Response: ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs okay. You‚Äôve been dealing with other things, so you haven‚Äôt been ready to look for changes‚Ä¶yet.‚Äù

Client 2: ‚ÄúI didn‚Äôt hear a thing you said.‚Äù

Response: ‚ÄúNot consciously. But you can be sure your unconscious did.‚Äù

Utilization is also used to minimize the effect of external disturbances (‚Äúand all the noise and activity out there can remind you how good it is to relax inside‚Äù), or to mind-read the client‚Äôs internal experience (‚ÄúThat‚Äôs right‚Ä¶‚Äù).

21. FACTIVE (AWARENESS) PREDICATES

Presupposing truth by the use of words such as: realize, know, become aware of, understand.

Examples:

‚ÄúHave you noticed that your body has begun to relax naturally?‚Äù

‚ÄúAs you become aware of your breathing slowing down, you start to feel more comfortable.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhen you realize that you can make changes, there‚Äôll be no stopping you.‚Äù

22. COMMENTARY ADJECTIVES AND ADVERBS

Words that predispose the listener to accept the quality of everything that follows, such as: kindly, usefully, surprisingly.

Examples:

‚ÄúHappily, you don‚Äôt have to do anything to relax. Just allow it to happen?‚Äù

‚ÄúInterestingly, your unconscious is very protective of you.‚Äù

‚ÄúClearly, your breathing has begun to settle down.‚Äù

This section is not a complete representation of all the structures I wrote about in Patterns of the Hypnotic Techniques of Milton H. Erickson, M.D. However, after becoming familiar with the ones outlined here, the reader will have many tools to create pervasive change. By ‚Äúbecoming familiar,‚Äù I mean to suggest that the reader should write out as many examples of each pattern as he or she can, then create various inductions out of these examples. Spend a day or two on each pattern. Also, practice delivering them aloud‚Äîto a friend, a voice recorder, or even your dog. Practicing hypnotic inductions in front of a mirror can be extremely state-altering.

RESOURCE FILE 6

Eliciting and Annotating Strategies

FORMAL ELICITATION OF A strategy requires systematic questioning and observation. Pay special attention to eye-accessing cues in relation to spoken replies to your questions. Strategies usually operate out of conscious awareness, and eye-accessing cues may reveal hidden aspects that need further investigation.

1. Begin by asking someone who is able to do something you would like to learn how they do it. Listen for sensory-specific information‚Äîthat is, what they see, hear, and feel. Make sure they don‚Äôt start too far into the strategy. Keep asking, ‚ÄúWhat happens just before that?‚Äù until the starting point has been identified.

2. When the starting point has been identified, keep asking, ‚ÄúAnd what happens next?‚Äù until the strategy is complete. Use the following symbols to record the answers.

Visual = V

Auditory = A

Kinesthetic = K

Recalled = r

Constructed = c

Digital = d

Internal to the subject = i

External to the subject = e

Transitioning = >

Examples:

The person who felt he couldn‚Äôt draw was using the following strategy:

Vr > Ar > K > Ar > Vr > K (recalling the sight and sound of seeing and hearing his critical teacher, feeling bad, recalling his father‚Äôs criticisms, feeling bad), which then looped back to the beginning, creating the feeling that there was no alternative to his problem situation.

The person who felt he could draw was using the following strategy:

Ve > K > Vc > K > Ve > K (studying the scene, feeling and seeing the ‚Äúwire,‚Äù tracing details of the scene, seeing and feeling the marks were ‚Äúright‚Äù).
